ii
Design of a twin automatic gain control amplifier in 180nm CMOS
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
AGC amplifier is commonly used in electronic systems that deal with input signal of wide
dynamic range.
A twin automatic gain control(AGC) amplifier is designed in 0.18um CMOS technology
for a data acquisition system. The specification requires a discrete gain setting of 1 to
64 with a step of 2, high linearity, low current consumption, input referred thermal noise
lower than a certain level.
As a low frequency application with discrete gain control settings, close-loop fully differ-
ential PGA together with feedforward AGC topology is chosen for the design, gain adjust-
ment is achieved by switches connected in series with input resistors, by turning on or off
the switches, close-loop feedback factor is varied, and therfore accurate gain control can
be achieved. Gain control is achieved by using envelope detector to track peak value of
input signal and larger envelope signal of the 2 input channels is sent to comparator to
compare with different reference voltages to generate the desired gain. Multiple reference
voltages are generated using voltage regulator.
Desired AGC circuit operation is achieved, and the designed twin AGC amplifier circuit
can provide discrete gain settings of 1, 2, 4, 8, 16, 32, 64 based on input signal level. High
linearity PGA is designed with a total harmonic distortion of less than 0.025%. The largest
input referred thermal noise of 68 nV
√
Hz presents when gain=2. Current consumption of the
whole system is around 230µA.
Design of a twin automatic gain control amplifier in 180nm CMOS

Design of a twin automatic gain control amplifier in 180nm CMOS
==PAGE:13==
1
Introduction
1.1
Automatic gain control (AGC) amplifier
Automatic gain control (AGC) amplifier is an important building block for analog signal
processing, and finds application in both high frequency and low frequency devices and
systems. AGC amplifier can adjust the output signal amplitude automatically to adapt to
the input signal that has a large dynamic range.
Originally, AGC circuit was invented for radio receiver in the last century. Nowadays,
AGC amplifier plays an essential role in systems that need to deal with input signal of
wide dynamic range.
An automatic gain control amplifier circuit typically consists of 3 basic blocks, an ampli-
fier with adjustable gain, for example variable gain amplifier(VGA) or programmable gain
amplifier(PGA), a envelope detector or peak detector to measure the envelope of input
or output signal, a control circuit that generates gain control signal to adjust the gain of
VGA/PGA, for example a comparator.
1.2
Objectives
The aim of this thesis is to design a low frequency application twin automatic gain control
amplifier in 180nm CMOS technology for a data acquisition system that is able to auto-
matically adjust the gain based on input signal amplitude, there are 2 channels of input
signal, the one with larger envelope value is used to determine the overall gain of the
whole system. To have low signal distortion, a PGA that has a high linearity and accurate
gain settings needs to be designed. To make sure the gain setting is accurate, the enve-
lope detector needs to be accurate and sensitive to signal with low amplitude. Hysteresis
comparator needs to be designed so that noisy signal or frequent input signal level inter-
section of 2 channels don’t cause the mis-triggering of comparator and resulting in a noisy
output.
1.3
Thesis organization
The first chapter briefly introduces the concept of AGC amplifier, and the design objec-
tives of this thesis. The second chapter has a review of the essential building blocks of
AGC amplifier, their current state of the art topology. The third chapter discusses the
complete system designed in this project, detailed design process of each block, and
pre-simulation results under nominal condition are presented in this chapter. The fourth
chapter discusses the layout considerations and methodology, and presents the post-
layout simulation results under nominal condition. In the end the chapter, a comparison
of the of pre-simulation and post-layout simulation results is made. The fifth chapter talks
about the conclusion and have an envision of future work. The appendix collects all the
pre-simulation results and post-layout simulation results of PVT corners and monte carlo
simulation. Figures of schematic and layout are also in appendix.

2
Review of automatic gain control(AGC)
amplifier
Automatic gain control amplifier can mainly be categorized into feedback and feed-forward
types. For AGC amplifier of feedback topology, it generally consists of Variable gain am-
plifier(VGA) or programmable gain amplifier(PGA), envelope detector and control signal
generation circuit as illustrated in fig. 2.1, the envelope detector measures the envelope
of output signal which determines the gain of PGA/VGA. In the feedback design, a longer
settling time is introduced results from the propagation delay of PGA/VGA, and stability
performance is also a major concern. As the envelope detector measures output signal,
which has larger amplitude than input signal, the design requirements for the sensitivity
and accuracy of envelope detector is eased [1].
Vout
Vin
PGA/
VGA
Envelope
detector
Control
circuit
Figure 2.1: AGC loop with feedback topology
For AGC amplifier of feedforward topology, it basically consists the same circuit blocks as
AGC amplifier using feedabck topology, the difference is that the envelope signal which
is used to determine the gain is taken directly from input signal instead of output signal
as illustrated in fig. 2.2. As the envelope is taken from input signal, during the process
of of control signal generation, it does not go through the PGA/VGA propagation delay
like the feedback topology does, and therefore this topology has a faster settling time.
But the feed-forward topology adds more requirements for the accuracy and sensitivity
of envelope detector circuit as it needs to measure input signal which has very small
amplitude [2].

2.1
Programmabler gain amplifier(PGA)
A lot of applications require the use of amplifiers with adjustable gain which is called Ad-
justable Gain Amplifier (AGA), and it includes 2 different types, Variable Gain Amplifiers
(VGA) and Programmable Gain Amplifiers (PGA). VGA is a type of amplifier that pro-
vides continuous gain range, and PGA is a type of amplifier that provides discrete gain
changing range. VGA and PGA topology and architectures for high frequency and low
frequency can be quite different, open-loop topology are more popular for high frequency
applications as they have advantages of low noise, low power, but the disadvantage is
weak linearity compared to close-loop amplifiers, close-loop topology are popular in low
frequency VGA/PGA applications due to their high linearity and simple and accurate gain
control [3]. As this project is a low frequency application, only PGA and VGA topology for
low frequency applications are discussed here in this section.
2.1.1
Resistor array PGA
One basic method of realizing close-loop PGA with discrete gain is using an array with
resistors connected in series with MOS switches, and the equivalent resistance can be
varied by turning on and off switches like illustrated in fig. 2.3, the feedback factor is
changed by controlling the ratio of Rf/Rin and therefore obtaining different close-loop
gain, high linearity and accurate gain settings can be achieved if the loop gain is de-
signed to be large enough and the resistor network is linear enough, as the close-loop
gain depends on the ratio of input and feedback resistors but not the single resistor value,
the gain settings can be robust under different process and temperature variation [4].
4

2.1.2
Switched capacitor array PGA
This method is similar to the resistor array PGA topology by replacing the resistors with
capacitors, which occupy less area and have less uncertainty compared to resistor, but
the price is complexity and extra clock signal generation circuit is needed to control the
switches.The gain of this topology is decided by capacitor ratio Cin/Cf, the gain can be
tuned by changing the value of Cf just like the way used in resistor array PGA [5]. A
simplified circuit illustrating the switched capacitor PGA is shown in fig. 2.4.

2.1.3
Degenerated differential pair with variable degeneration resistor
A simple VGA and source degenerated differential pair with variable degeneration resis-
tor implemented with a MOS operating in triode region are shown in fig. 2.5. The gain of
open-loop based VGA is controlled by a variable transconductance gm or load resistance
RL, in the degenerated differential pair VGA topology, the gain can be varied continuously
by controlling the gate voltage of source degeneration MOS, it has the advantages of de-
manding low power while presenting low noise which is popular in high frequency appli-
cations, but the drawback is the weak linearity and gain accuracy compared to close-loop
topology. However, if low power consumption is more desired over linearity, open-loop
topology can also be implemented for low frequency applications [3].

2.1.4
Tunable Pseudo-Resistor based VGA
The tunable pseudo-resistor based VGA topology is a close-loop VGA implementation
but similar to the resistor array PGA topology, instead of using switches to get discrete
resistance and therefore discrete gain, this topology uses a cross-coupled depletion-mode
NMOS as pseudo input resistor to set the gain by controlling the gate voltage and together
with switchable feedback resistors. Linear and continuous gain control can be achieved
with this topology, but the drawback of this method is increased distortion level [7]. The
circuit diagram is illustrated in fig. 2.6.

Based on the high linearity, high gain accuracy, design complexity and the project require-
ments concern, the resistor array PGA topology is chosen in this design.

2.2
Envelope detector
Envelope detector, also known as peak detector and sample hold(PDSH) circuit, is an
important circuit element in analog signal processing that tracks the envelope or peak
value of the input signal, and it’s widely used automatic gain control loop applications.
2.2.1
Simple RC diode envelope detector
An envelope detector is typically built with rectifying element and holding element, the
simplest envelope detector is the one with just a diode, a capacitor and a resistor like
illustrated in fig. 2.7.
Vout
C
R
Vin
Figure 2.7: RC diode envelope detector
The input signal is rectified by diode, and therefore only positive half-cycles of the input
signal appear at the diode output. On each positive cycle of input signal, the filter capacitor
C is charged to the peak value of input signal, with the relatively small time-constant
formed by the capacitor and the low output resistance of the signal source plus the on-
resistance of diode. During the negative cycle, the diode is reverse-biased and non-
conducting, the capacitor discharges slowly through high load resistance. The values of
capacitor and resistor are chosen high enough to smooth out the high frequency ripple(the
RC value should be design to be RC >>
1
ωinputsignal ), but low enough that the output
voltage will follow the envelope change of input signal to avoid inertia distortion(RC <
1
ωenvelopefrequency ).
2.2.2
Active envelope detector
As in practical situation, the diode is non-ideal and there is a voltage drop on it, a simple
diode envelope detector is not accurate enough, therefore an operational amplifier is used
to configure the circuit to be a positive rectifying voltage follower to increase the accuracy
of envelope detector as illustrated in fig. 2.8. The operation of this topology is the same
as the basic circuit, when the input signal increases, the output signal follows and charge
the hold capacitor until input signal reaches it’s peak value, and when input signal is
decreasing, the output signal will also try to decrease by discharging through the load
resistor which has a large resistance value [8].
Design of a twin automatic gain control amplifier in 180nm CMOS

==PAGE:20==

Figure 2.8: Active envelope detector
2.2.3
Modified active envelope detector
To better implement active envelope detector in CMOS technology, the diode can be re-
placed by a PMOS transistor, as illustrated in fig. 2.9. When input voltage is higher than
hold voltage, the output level of operational amplifier is low, which turns on the PMOS and
the capacitor is charged, when input signal is decreasing, the output of operational ampli-
fier is high and PMOS is off, the hold voltage also decreases by discharging through load
resistor. However, this configuration may exhibit unstable response and needs proper
compensation [9].
Venvelope
C
R
Vin
GND
VDD
Figure 2.9: Modified active envelope detector
2.2.4
Current-mirror compensated envelope detector
As the OTA used for envelope detector has a finite bandwidth, the classical active enve-
lope detector may have a critically damped or even unstable performance, and therefore
proper compensation techniques are needed [9]. To better compensate the loop stability,
a envelope detector topology that uses current-mirror like compensation technique has
been proved to be effective by [10]. The circuit is illustrated in fig. 2.10.

2.3
Comparator
Comparator is a circuit that compares 2 signals and switches output binary state based
on the comparison result. There are mainly 2 types of comparator, open-loop comparator
and positive feedback comparator [11].
2.3.1
Hysteresis comparator
When comparator is at a noisy environment or the amplitude of input signals have intersec-
tion point frequently in a short period, a comparator with hysteresis property is needed.
Hysteresis is the quality of the comparator that the output switches state at 2 different
threshold points, for example if initially the voltage at the positive terminal of a hysteresis
is lower than voltage at negative terminal, as the positive terminal voltage increases and
reaches the positive threshold point, the comparator switches state from 0 to 1, after this
point, when positive terminal voltage decreases and drops lower than the positive thresh-
old point, comparator does not switch state until positive terminal voltage drops below
the negative threshold point, the voltage difference between the positive and negative
threshold point is the hysteresis voltage as illustrated in fig.2.11.
Design of a twin automatic gain control amplifier in 180nm CMOS

The 2 envelope detectors measure 2 channels of input signal and the envelopes signals
are sent to the maximum signal selection circuit, the larger envelope signal is selectee
and routed to the input port of gain control signal generation circuit, which is comprise of
6 hysteresis comparators. Once the input signal is lower or higher than a certain level,
the corresponding comparators will switch state and turn on or off the switches of PGA to
adjust the gain. The reference voltages that are needed by comparators are generated
by a reference voltage generation circuit, which is built with a simple voltage regulator.
The detailed operation and design process for each blocks is discussed in following sec-
tions of this chapter.

3.1.1.2
Common mode feedback
For differential amplifier using MOS current source as load, the current in PMOS current
source and NMOS current source might be in conflict as mismatches from PMOS and
NMOS current mirrors that define tail current ISS and ID3,4 create an error between ISS
2
and ID3,4, which might cause M3, M4 or M9 entering triode region [6]. Therefore, common
mode feedback(CMFB) circuit is needed to stabilize and make sure the output common
mode voltage is midrail of power supply voltage( VDD
2 ).
To design the CMFB circuit for 2-stage miller compensation OTA, there are some different
methods. One is to sense the output common mode voltage, and return the result to the
first stage, but this method can result in some stability issue as it is a long feedback loop,
which has many poles involved. Anther method is to use simple resistive CMFB for both
input and output stages, this method can avoid the stability issue, but with the price of
decreasing gain and increasing area, and the output common mode voltage might not be
accurately VDD
2
[6].
In this design, to avoid the instability resulted from CMFB circuit sensing output common
mode voltage and feedback to first stage, and to not degrading the gain, a simple resistive
CMFB loop using MOS pseudo resistor is used for the first stage output common mode
level stabilization, and output common mode voltage is sensed by 2 resistors and an error
amplifier and feedback to gate of current source load M7, M8 of second stage.
To analysis the stability of the designed common mode feedback loop, the common mode
equivalent circuit is drawn and illustrated in fig. 3.5.
20
Design of a twin automatic gain control amplifier in 180nm CMOS

As the CMFB error amplfier form a negative loop together with the active load transistors

without compensation, as it has been simulated in cadence that the worst phase margin
Design of a twin automatic gain control amplifier in 180nm CMOS
21
==PAGE:34==
is around 20◦under different PVT corners without compensation. Different techniques of
compensating the CMFB loop have been investigated during simulation, and it is found
effective by adding compensation capacitors between the CMFB output node and the 2
output nodes of main amplifier as illustrated in fig. 3.5, the phase margin can be improved
to around 60◦under different PVT corners and therefore stability is ensured, but the price
is the bandwidth of main amplifier is slightly decreased.
The DC gain of CMFB loop is found to be:

than the unity gain bandwidth to ensure a good phase margin.
Choose the GBW of the error amplifier to be half of the second pole frequency GBWCMFB =
13MHz, which should result in a phase margin of around 60◦, and the bandwidth is
close to the designed main amplifier bandwidth. To make sure the CMFB compensa-
tion capacitors don’t degrade the bandwidth of the main amplifier too much, the compen-
sation capacitors should be chosen to have a small value, as an initial guess, choose
CCMFB = 0.08pF.

3.1.1.3
Resistor array PGA
After the core amplifier is designd, it can be combined with resistors and transistor switches
that works in triode region, and then a resistor array PGA is formed, the circuit is illustrated
in fig. 3.6. As it can be seen in the figure, there are 6 resistors and PMOS transistors for
each side in corresponding to 6 gain settings(2 to 64 with a step of 2). PMOS is chosen
as the switch because it is turned on by low gate voltage, and therefore the gain setting
is more stable under different PVT conditions, as NMOS switch is tuned on by high gate
voltage and comparator output gain control voltage varies under different PVT voltage
variations, and therefore the on-resistance of the NMOS switch may vary a lot and impact
the gain accuracy. On the other hand, the size of MOS switch needs to be chosen large
enough to decrease the on-resistance and so as to increase gain setting accuracy.
The relationship between close-loop gain and feedback/input resistor ratio is
Gain =
Rfb
Rin,total
(3.39)
To fix the close-loop bandwidth of the PGA, 2 capacitors are added in parallel with each
feedback resistor to make the PGA also work as a low pass filter, the relationship between
the cutoff frequency and resistor and capacitor value is
f3dB =
1
2π · Rfb · Cfilter
(3.40)
Choose Rfb = 160kohm, and as the bandwidth should be f3dB = 100kHz, the capacitor
can be calculated
Design of a twin automatic gain control amplifier in 180nm CMOS
23
==PAGE:36==
Cfilter =
1
2π · Rfb · f3dB
= 9.9pF
(3.41)
The capacitor value is chosen to be Cfilter = 3.3pF eventually to have some margin,
taking into consideration that the bandwidth varies under different close-loop gain settings
and also in different PVT conditions as it can be seen in simulation results.

3.2
Envelope detector
The envelope detector is built with a differential to single-ended amplifier which converts
the input differential signal to single-ended signal and an envelope sample hold circuit
which tracks the envelope of its input signal. The differential to single-ended amplifier is
discussed first.
3.2.1
Differential to single-ended amplifier
The 2-stage miller compensation OTA topology is chosen to be the core of differential to
single-ended amplifier, as this topology can provide nearly rail-to-rail output signal swing.
The circuit diagram is illustrated in fig. 3.16.
Design of a twin automatic gain control amplifier in 180nm CMOS

3.2.1.1
Circuit design procedure
As the topology chosen for the differential to single-ended amplifier is quite similar to
the PGA core, both are 2-stage miller compensation OTA, the design methodology and
procedure is the same, but without the consideration for CMFB circuit, as single-ended
output amplifier has its output common mode voltage level forced to stable by negative
feedback.
First, a specification for the 2 stage OTA is made. The close loop gain is 1. Take GBW =
2MHz which is larger than desired system bandwidth 100kHz and have some margin, so
as to make sure the amplifier has fast enough response. Slew rate is set to be SR = 2 V
µs
which is sufficient for input signal of 100kHz while not consuming too much current. Phase
margin is designed to be PM = 70◦. As this amplifier is not in the main signal path, noise
is not the major concern.
The OTA transfer function is
AV (s) =
AV (0) · (1 −
s
ωz )
(1 +
s
ωp1 ) · (1 +
s
ωp2 )
(3.44)
where
AV (0) = A1ststage · A2ndstage = gm1 · gm6 · (ro3//ro1) · (ro6//ro7),
ωz = gm6
Cc ,
ωp1 =
1
gm6·(ro3//ro1)·(ro6//ro7)·Cc ,
ωp2 = gm6
CL .
To get a phase margin of 70◦, the zero and second pole position are designed to be
ωz = 10ωu and ωp2 = 4ωu.
Then the miller capacitor value can be derived, assuming load capacitance is CL = 1.5pF.
ωz = 10 · ωu →gm6
Cc
= 10 · gm1
Cc
→gm6 = 10gm1
(3.45)
30

3.2.1.2
Close-loop topology
To make the designed 2-stage OTA a differential to single-ended amplifier, it can be con-
nected into close-loop form as illustrated in fig. 3.17.

vout
vin+ −vin−
= R4
R3
(3.63)
When R1
R2 = R3
R4 .
As the largest input differential level is 800mV , to avoid distortion due to limited power
supply voltage, the close-loop gain is set to be 1. And because it is an unbuffered OTA,
the feedback resistor value needs to be chosen as large, otherwise the loop gain would be
decreased and it results in signal distortion. Adding buffer circuit would increase current
consumption, trade-offs need to be made between current consumption and area when
using larger resistors.
To avoid consuming more current, buffer circuit is not added. The feedback resistor value
is chosen to be R4 = R3 = 160Kohm, which make the amplifier has high enough linearity.
And the other 2 resistor values are chosen to be R1 = R2 = 2Kohm.

3.2.2
Envelope detector
3.2.2.1
Circuit design procedure
The envelope detector circuit topology used in this design is comprise of an OTA, a current
mirror pair driven by OTA as the rectifying component, a capacitor and a transistor made
pseudo resistor(to provide large resistance) as the holding component. Using current
mirror in replacement of a diode as the rectification element can effectively optimize the
linearity performance. Unlike a lot of typical envelope detector circuits, buffer circuit is not
needed here as the output will be connect to the gate of a MOS transistor, so there is no
output load impact. The circuit diagram is illustrated in fig. 3.26.
The operation of this circuit can be explained as, when input voltage which is connected
to the negative terminal of OTA is higher than output voltage, the current in M2 increases
and the current mirror is switched on and the increased current in M2 is mirrored to M8
to charge the capacitor C1 until the output voltage reaches the input voltage level. When
output voltage becomes higher than input voltage, current in M2 decreases and the cur-
rent mirror is off, hold capacitor starts discharging through the pseudo resistor M9 until it
drops back to the same voltage level as the input signal.
Venvelope
OTA
Vin
VDD
M7
M8
M9
Pseudo resistor
C1
(a) Envelope detector circuit diagram
VDD
Vin+
M1
M3
M5
M4
Vin-
Vout
M2
IBIAS
M6
(b) OTA used in (a)
Figure 3.26: Envelope detector circuit diagram
The accuracy of the envelope detector is limited by non-idealities of the rectifying com-
ponents and the error amplifier. The non-idealities from error amplifier is mainly because
of limited DC gain, CMRR, offset voltage and slew rate, meanwhile there is a quiescent
current flowing in the current mirror even when it’s turned off, this results in a small offset
voltage at the output of envelope detector [10]. Therefore, to make sure the accuracy of
envelope detector, the parameters mentioned above should be properly designed.
The DC gain of this circuit from input to output can be expressed as
A0 = gm2 · (rds2//rds4// 1
gm7
) · gm8 · (rds8//rds9)
(3.64)
To increase the gain, the transconductance of M2 can be increased, but with the price
of larger current consumption. The length of M8 can be enlarged to increase output
resistance, but with the price of taking more area. Meanwhile, increasing the DC gain too
much may result in decreasing of phase margin.
Firstly, the OTA for envelope detector is designed. Choose GBW = 2MHz, which is
higher than the desired bandwidth 100kHz of the system to have enough margin, SR =

3.3
Hysteresis comparator
3.3.1
Circuit design procedure
The 2-stage open-loop hysteresis comparator with internal feedback transistors topology
is chosen in this design as it provides simple hysteresis voltage adjustment and takes
less area compared to hysteresis topology using external resistor feedback which needs
large feedback resistor. Based on the internal feedback hysteresis comparator topology
discussed in chapter 2, output stage is implemented in the topology to provide sufficient
output voltage swing. The circuit diagram is shown in fig. 3.32.
VDD
Vin-
Vout
M1

M12
M11
M10
M9
M5
M4
M8
Vin+
M2
Figure 3.32: Hysteresis comparator circuit diagram [11]
To design the hysteresis comparator, there are several parameters that need to be taken
into consideration. The propagation delay time tp which is defined as the time difference
between input excitation and output response. This parameter is mainly decided by cur-
rent, output voltage swing and node capacitance [11].
The resolution of a comparator is the minimum change of voltage at the input of compara-
tor that causes the state switching at the ouput, it can be expressed as
V inmin = VOH −VOL
Av(0)
(3.75)
Where VOH and VOL is the highest and lowest voltage level at output.
Set the propagation delay time to be tp = 2µs, comparator resolution V inmin = 2mV , the
highest and lowest output voltage level VOH = 1.78V and VOL = 0.02V . Assume the load
capacitance is CL = 1.5pF.
The current in M8−11 can be calculated based on its relationship with propagation delay
time:
ID8,9,10,11 = CL · dvout
dt

tp
= 1.32µA
(3.76)

As illustrated in fig. 3.33a, assume that a positive reference voltage is added at the gate
of M1, and voltage level at the gate of M2 is less than reference voltage initially, at the
moment M1, 3, 6 is on and M2, 4, 7 is off, all the current from M5 is flowing through M1,
and vo2 is high. When vin is increasing toward the threshold voltage, the current flowing
through M2 is increasing until input voltage reaches the trigger point and the current flows
in M2 and M6 is equal, beyond the trigger point, the output of comparator is flipped. The
point where ID2 = ID6 can be used to roughly estimate the triggering point voltage [11].
The relationship between current in M1, M3, M2 and M6 is

3.3.2
Maximum envelope signal selection circuit
The maximum envelope signal selection circuit is built with 1 hysteresis comparator and 2
analog switches. When envelope signal 1 is larger than envelope signal 2, the comparator
output voltage level is high and the upper switch is on, envelope signal 1 is bypassed to
output and determines the PGA gain. When envelope signal 2 is larger than envelope
signal 1, the comparator output voltage level is low and the other switch is on and envelope
signal 2 is bypassed to output to determine the gain. Hysteresis comparator can effectively
avoid situation of output state frequently switching when 2 envelope signal values are
close and having intersections frequently. The circuit diagram is shown in fig. 3.34.
Vswitch
Venvelope1
Venvelope
Venvelope2
GND
VDD
Figure 3.34: Maximum envelope signal selection circuit diagram
46
Design of a twin automatic gain control amplifier in 180nm CMOS
==PAGE:59==
3.3.3
Gain control signal generation circuit
The gain control signal generation circuit is formed by using 6 hysteresis comparators
designed in this section, and connecting the comparator positive input terminal to the
envelope signal from maximum envelope signal selection circuit, connecting comparator
negative input terminal to 6 different reference voltages. When input envelope signal is
higher or lower than a certain reference voltage level, the comparator switches state and
turns on or off the corresponding switch of PGA and accurate gain control is achieved.
The circuit diagram is shown in fig. 3.35.

Hysteresis voltage simulation
The hysteresis voltage is tested by adding a slowly varying ramp signal to the positive
input terminal of comparator, and adding a reference voltage of 0.9V to the negative input
terminal, the result is illustrated in fig. 3.36 and fig. 3.37. It can be seen from the presented
figures that the hysteresis voltage in nominal condition is 8.3mV .

3.4
Reference voltage generation circuit
3.4.1
Circuit design procedure
A voltage regulator circuit is used to generate accurate and PVT stable reference voltages
for the comparator, as the output of this circuit is connect to the gate of the input MOS of
comparator, driving capability of this circuit is not a major concern. The circuit diagram is
shown in fig. 3.38, the OTA used here is the same as the one in fig. 3.26b. To improve
the loop stability, a compensation capacitor is added in between the OTA output node and
drain node of M7.

3.5
Bypass circuit
Bypass circuit is used to bypass input signal to output directly when gain=1, as the in-
put referred noise contributed from PGA can be relatively big when PGA close-loop gain
setting is less than 2, therefore to avoid the noise contribution from PGA when gain is 1,
bypass circuit is used here. The bypass circuit can be built with simple analog switches
like shown in fig. 3.42.
52
Design of a twin automatic gain control amplifier in 180nm CMOS
==PAGE:65==
Vin+
VOPGA+
VO+
VOPGA-
VO-
Vin-
VGAINCTRL2
Figure 3.42: Bypass circuit
When VGAINCTRL = 1 and gain = 1, the 2 analog switches on the left side is on, the
2 analog switches on the right side is off, and input signal is bypassed to output. When
VGAINCTRL = 0 and gain ≥2, the 2 analog switches on the right side is on, the 2 analog
switches on the left side is off, the output signal of PGA is bypassed to output.

==PAGE:69==
4
Layout considerations and post-layout
verification
4.1
Layout considerations
To reduce mismatch in layout, below layout rules are followed.
4.1.1
Common-centroid layout
For the overall layout of the system, and layout of each blocks, components should be
placed as symmetrical as possible. For transistor layout, transistor fingers should be
ordered interdigitated with the fingers of the other transistor that needs to be matched, so
that a linear gradient across the integrated circuit in any device parameter, such as the
temperature or the gate-oxide thickness, has zero net effect on device performance [13].
The transistor devices can be placed in orders like ABBA or ABAB.
4.1.2
Dummy
To limit systematic variations, dummies devices should be added around all 4 edges of
matched transistors.
4.1.3
Guard ring
Matched transistors should be surrounded by guard rings to ensure good connection to
the substrate.
In this design, layout for each blocks and system layout are conducted following the rules
mentioned above. Figures that show layout details are presented in appendix. In the
following sections of this chapter, mainly the post-layout simulation results are discussed.

==PAGE:81==
5
Conclusion and future work
5.1
Conclusion
AGC amplifier is a commonly used block in a lot of electronic systems that need to deal
with input signal that has wide dynamic range. This thesis project focuses on designing
a twin automatic gain control amplifier for a low frequency application.
The work that is done in the thesis project is:
Introducing the basic concept of AGC amplifier and it’s implementation. Researching of
different AGC amplifier structures and topology, and there application. A typical AGC am-
plifier includes an amplifier with adjustable gain, an envelope detector that tracks the peak
value of input or output signal, a gain control circuit which has a lot of different implemen-
tations based on the applications and requirements. Each blocks and their current state
of the art are introduced in chapter 2. It is found that an amplifier with adjustable gain
can be implemented in open-loop topology or close-loop topology, the gain adjustment of
open-loop topology is achieved by varying it’s transconductance or load resistance, the
gain adjustment of close-loop topology is achieved by varying it’s close-loop feedback
factor. Close-loop topology is more stable and insensitive to PVT variations and suitable
for low frequency application compared to open-loop topology which is more suitable for
high frequency design. Therefore, close-loop fully differential amplifier is chosen to build
the PGA in this project.
The whole AGC amplifier system is built using the feedforward AGC topology, which is
suitable for the discrete gain settings implementation. The envelope detector measures
differential input signal envelope, and maximum envelope selection circuit selects the
larger envelope signal, which is then routed to the gain control circuit to generate appro-
priate gain control signal for PGA. The input signal is bypassed to output directly when
gain is 1 to avoid the noise contribution from PGA, when gain is larger, input referred
thermal noise decreases to acceptable level.
A fully differential programmable gain amplifier with discrete gain settings of 1 to 64 is de-
signed. To stabilize the output common mode level, a classic CMFB circuit is implemented
in the design, which uses simple resister output common mode sensing technique and
a 5-transistor OTA as the error amplifier, compensation technique is used to make sure
the CMFB loop is stable. The core amplifier topology is chosen to be 2-stage miller com-
pensation OTA which can provide sufficient open-loop gain and offers large output swing.
The designed PGA has high linearity performance and acceptable noise performance,
and consumes around 70µA current under nominal test condition.
An envelope detector is designed, which is built with a differential to single-ended ampli-
fier that converts differential input signal to single-ended signal for the envelope detector
sample holding circuit. 2-stage miller compensation OTA is also chosen as the core am-
plifier for the differential to single-ended signal conversion in favour of it’s large output
swing. The envelope sample and hold circuit is designed with a 5-transistor OTA and a
current mirror as the rectifying component, a capacitor and a NMOS pseudo resistor(up
to 40Gohm) are used as the holding and discharging components. The envelope mea-
surement accuracy is acceptable, there is a small offset voltage around 0.9mV results
from the limited gain of error amplifier and a quiescent current from current mirror which
causes the small offset.
Design of a twin automatic gain control amplifier in 180nm CMOS
69
==PAGE:82==
A hysteresis comparator with internal positive feedback is designed, the hysteresis can be
adjusted by varying the internal positive feedback transistor size, which is implemented
for the input stage. Output stage is implemented to achieve acceptable output voltage
swing.
A simple reference voltage generation circuit is built with voltage regulator topology to pro-
vide reference voltages to the comparators to generate desired gain control signals. The
reference voltage generation circuit can provide accurate and stable reference voltages
under different PVT variations.
5.2
Future work
During the 5 months of working on analog integrated circuit design and AGC amplifier
design, I find it even more interesting and worth effort, and I want to learn and explore
more in this field.
For the design of AGC amplifier in this project, there are things that need to be further
optimized:
Due to some late design change, the system layout needs to be adjusted based on the last
completed version, and it’s not updated yet. Therefore, the system post-layout simulation
result is collected using the layout netlist result of each block but not as a whole complete
layout. Further work can be done for system layout.
For the current design of PGA, eventhough capacitors are added in parallel with the feed-
back resistor so that it works as a low pass filter to fix the bandwidth of the amplifier, the
bandwidth still varies with changing gain. Methods need to be investigated in how to build
a PGA with constant bandwidth. The layout of PGA also needs to be optimized, as it can
be seen from the post-layout result that there is a DC gain drop and bandwidth drop after
layout. As in the current design, the input signal has to be biased to output to avoid noise
contribution from PGA when gain=1, and further investigation can be done in decreasing
the noise so that bypass circuit can be removed and it meets the noise specification even
when gain=1.
A VGA that has the capability of providing continuous gain setting for low frequency ap-
plication is also of interesting, as most of the VGA circuits are open-loop topology for
high frequency applications, therefore high linearity VGA for low frequency use is worth
investigation.
An AGC amplifier with feedback topology is also interesting and more commonly used,
as in the beginning of the project, the feedback AGC topology was investigated but didn’t
work as expected, further work can be done in researching and building a feedback topol-
ogy AGC amplifier that has continuous gain setting for low frequency application.
In the current design of envelope detector, a simple 5-transistor OTA which has a relatively
low DC gain is used as the error amplifier, and investigations can be made in using OTA
with higher DC gain to increase the accuracy and meanwhile maintain a satisfying loop
stability.

Design of a twin automatic gain control amplifier in 180nm CMOS

System layout

sign change, an update of system layout is not done yet, all the single blocks layout are
done. Therefore the system post-layout simulation results are collected using schematic
simulation but with each block using layout netlist.

A.13
Project management
Following the plan, 1 month was used for literature review and paper research. More than
2 months were used for system design and each circuit block design, 1 month was used
for layout design, around 3 weeks were used for thesis writing.
